\section{Finite Gram-Matrix Test for Microscopic \(SU(2)_L\) Chirality Selection}

\subsection{Short Korean explanation}

The weak-bundle problem is no longer to invent \(SU(2)\).
The actual open problem is whether the emergent weak rank-two bundle lies entirely in the protected left-handed massless sector after mirror lifting.

This can be reduced to two \(2\times2\) overlap matrices.

\subsection{1. Input already available}

Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item a rank-two weak bundle \(\mathcal E_W\) is identified inside the low-energy zero-mode bundle \(\mathcal E_0\), with local orthonormal frame
\[
\Psi_A(X),\qquad A=1,2;
\]
\item the mirror-lifting mechanism has removed the vectorlike sector, so the massless chiral decomposition is
\[
Z_L = Z_{\mathrm{top}},\qquad Z_R = 0
\]
at the protected infrared level, or more generally
\[
Z_L=Z_{\mathrm{top}}\oplus Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L,\qquad Z_R=Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^R
\]
before lifting and only \(Z_{\mathrm{top}}\) remains after full-rank pairing. 
\end{enumerate}

Then the unresolved question is whether the weak doublet \(\mathcal E_W\) is carried by \(Z_{\mathrm{top}}\) and not by \(Z_R\). 

\subsection{2. Chiral projectors}

Let
\[
P_L:\mathcal E_0\to Z_{\mathrm{top}},
\qquad
P_R:\mathcal E_0\to Z_R
\]
denote the orthogonal projectors onto the protected left-handed sector and the right-handed sector.

For a local orthonormal weak frame \(\Psi_A\), define the two Hermitian Gram matrices
\begin{equation}
G^{(L)}_{AB}
:=
\langle \Psi_A,\; P_L \Psi_B\rangle,
\qquad
A,B=1,2,
\label{eq:GL_def}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
G^{(R)}_{AB}
:=
\langle \Psi_A,\; P_R \Psi_B\rangle.
\label{eq:GR_def}
\end{equation}

These are finite \(2\times2\) matrices.

\subsection{3. Exact criterion for \(SU(2)_L\)}

The weak bundle lies entirely in the protected left-handed sector if and only if
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G^{(R)}=0,
\qquad
\det G^{(L)}\neq 0.
}
\label{eq:Gram_test_main}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Proof.}
\(G^{(R)}=0\) is equivalent to
\[
\langle \Psi_A,P_R\Psi_B\rangle=0
\qquad
\forall A,B,
\]
hence \(P_R\Psi_A=0\) for both frame vectors, so \(\mathcal E_W\cap Z_R=0\).

Meanwhile \(\det G^{(L)}\neq 0\) means that the two projected vectors \(P_L\Psi_1,P_L\Psi_2\) remain linearly independent, so the full rank-two weak bundle survives inside \(Z_{\mathrm{top}}\).
Thus
\[
\mathcal E_W\subset Z_{\mathrm{top}},
\qquad
\mathcal E_W\cap Z_R=0.
\]
Conversely, if these bundle-inclusion conditions hold, then \(G^{(R)}=0\) and \(G^{(L)}\) has rank \(2\), hence nonzero determinant. \(\square\)

\subsection{4. Operator form}

The same test can be written in connection form.
Let \(W_\mu\) be the weak Berry connection on \(\mathcal E_W\). Then genuine \(SU(2)_L\) chirality selection is equivalent to
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
P_R W_\mu P_R = 0,
\qquad
P_R W_\mu P_L = 0,
\qquad
P_L W_\mu P_L \neq 0
}
\label{eq:operator_form_main}
\end{equation}
on the protected low-energy sector.

But the most practical microscopic test is still \eqref{eq:Gram_test_main}, because it only requires the weak-bundle basis and the chiral projectors.

\subsection{5. Why this is the correct next step}

The weak-bundle theorem already gives the geometric precursor:
a rank-two weak bundle with \(u(2)\)-valued Berry connection, reducible to \(su(2)\) when the determinant bundle is trivialized. :contentReference[oaicite:5]{index=5}

The conditional fermion-multiplet theorem also makes clear that chiral selection is a separate input:
one must remove vectorlike doubling and retain the required chiral sector. :contentReference[oaicite:6]{index=6}

Therefore the genuinely missing electroweak step is not geometric \(U(2)\), but the finite projector test \eqref{eq:Gram_test_main}.

\subsection{6. Immediate executable procedure}

The next nonredundant calculation is:

\begin{enumerate}
\item choose a local orthonormal frame \(\Psi_1,\Psi_2\) of the candidate weak bundle \(\mathcal E_W\);
\item construct the protected chiral projector \(P_L\) and right-handed projector \(P_R\) from the actual defect zero-mode decomposition;
\item compute the \(2\times2\) matrices \(G^{(L)}\) and \(G^{(R)}\);
\item verify
\[
G^{(R)}=0,
\qquad
\det G^{(L)}\neq0.
\]
\end{enumerate}

If this holds, then the weak bundle is genuinely left-chiral:
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal E_W \cong \text{an }SU(2)_L\text{ doublet bundle.}
}
\]

\section{Final Summary}

The next unfinished Standard-Model step is fully reduced to a finite \(2\times2\) test.

For a local weak frame \(\Psi_A\), define
\[
G^{(L)}_{AB}=\langle \Psi_A,P_L\Psi_B\rangle,
\qquad
G^{(R)}_{AB}=\langle \Psi_A,P_R\Psi_B\rangle.
\]

Then microscopic \(SU(2)_L\) chirality selection is equivalent to
\[
\boxed{
G^{(R)}=0,
\qquad
\det G^{(L)}\neq0.
}
\]

So the remaining task is no longer conceptual:
it is the explicit computation of these two \(2\times2\) overlap matrices from the actual defect zero-mode bundle and the candidate weak subbundle.

\section{Reduction of the \(SU(2)_L\) Chirality Test to One Complex Determinant}

\subsection{Short Korean explanation}

After mirror lifting, the right-handed obstruction is gone.
So the weak-chirality problem reduces to whether the weak rank-two bundle \(\mathcal E_W\) projects with full rank into the protected left-handed sector \(Z_{\mathrm{top}}\).

This is equivalent to a single nonvanishing complex determinant.

\subsection{1. Protected left-handed basis and weak frame}

Let
\[
u_1,\ u_2
\]
be a local orthonormal basis of the relevant protected left-handed two-dimensional subspace inside \(Z_{\mathrm{top}}\) that is supposed to carry the weak doublet.

Let
\[
\Psi_1,\ \Psi_2
\]
be a local orthonormal frame of the candidate weak bundle \(\mathcal E_W\subset \mathcal E_0\).

Define the \(2\times2\) overlap matrix
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
U_{mA}:=\langle u_m,\Psi_A\rangle,
\qquad
m=1,2,\quad A=1,2.
}
\label{eq:U_overlap}
\end{equation}

\subsection{2. Relation to the Gram matrix}

The left-handed Gram matrix is
\begin{equation}
G^{(L)}_{AB}
=
\langle \Psi_A,P_L\Psi_B\rangle,
\label{eq:GL_repeat}
\end{equation}
where \(P_L\) is the projector onto the protected left-handed sector.

Since
\[
P_L=\sum_{m=1}^2 |u_m\rangle\langle u_m|
\]
on the relevant two-dimensional protected block, we get
\begin{equation}
G^{(L)}_{AB}
=
\sum_{m=1}^2
\langle \Psi_A,u_m\rangle
\langle u_m,\Psi_B\rangle
=
(U^\dagger U)_{AB}.
\label{eq:G_equals_UdagU}
\end{equation}

Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\det G^{(L)} = |\det U|^2.
}
\label{eq:detG_detU}
\end{equation}

So the weak bundle survives as a genuine left-handed rank-two doublet iff
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\det U\neq 0.
}
\label{eq:detU_main}
\end{equation}

\subsection{3. Interpretation}

\(\det U\neq0\) means exactly that the two weak-frame vectors \(\Psi_1,\Psi_2\) have linearly independent projections onto the protected left-handed sector.
Equivalently,
\[
\mathcal E_W \hookrightarrow Z_{\mathrm{top}}
\]
with full rank.

If \(\det U=0\), then the weak frame collapses to rank \(<2\) after projection, so it does not define a genuine left-handed \(SU(2)\) doublet bundle.

\subsection{4. Practical computation}

The microscopic \(SU(2)_L\) test is therefore:

\begin{enumerate}
\item compute the protected left-handed basis \(u_1,u_2\) from the actual defect PDE after mirror lifting;
\item compute the candidate weak frame \(\Psi_1,\Psi_2\) inside the \(Q\)-sector bundle;
\item evaluate
\[
U_{mA}=\langle u_m,\Psi_A\rangle;
\]
\item check
\[
\boxed{\det U\neq0.}
\]
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{5. Why this is the correct next open}

The notes already isolate the weak rank-two bundle problem as a separate finite lemma (\(\mathcal E_W\) as a rank-two isotypic component), and also explicitly state that identifying these modes with the actual Standard-Model chiral electroweak doublets is still unproved. 

Thus the next real electroweak computation is not conceptual.
It is the explicit evaluation of \(\det U\).

\section{Final Summary}

After mirror lifting, the \(SU(2)_L\) chirality-selection problem reduces to one complex determinant.

Define
\[
U_{mA}=\langle u_m,\Psi_A\rangle
\]
between a protected left-handed basis \(u_m\) and a weak-bundle frame \(\Psi_A\).
Then
\[
G^{(L)}=U^\dagger U,
\qquad
\det G^{(L)}=|\det U|^2.
\]

Therefore the exact finite criterion is
\[
\boxed{
\det U\neq0.
}
\]

If this holds, then the candidate weak rank-two bundle survives with full rank inside the protected left-handed sector, i.e. it is a genuine \(SU(2)_L\) doublet bundle.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Reduction of the \(SU(2)_L\) test from \(\det U\neq0\) to one scalar \(s\neq0\)}

\subsection{Short Korean explanation}

If both the candidate weak bundle \(\mathcal E_W\) and the relevant protected left-handed sector transform as the same multiplicity-one complex \(2\)-dimensional irreducible module, then the overlap matrix \(U\) is forced by representation theory to be a scalar multiple of the identity.
Therefore the full-rank test \(\det U\neq0\) reduces to checking one complex number \(s\neq0\).

\subsection{1. Starting point}

Let
\[
\Psi_1,\Psi_2
\]
be a local orthonormal frame of the candidate weak bundle \(\mathcal E_W\), and
\[
u_1,u_2
\]
a local orthonormal basis of the relevant protected left-handed \(2\)-dimensional sector inside \(Z_{\mathrm{top}}\).

Define the overlap matrix
\begin{equation}
U_{mA}:=\langle u_m,\Psi_A\rangle.
\label{eq:U_start_again}
\end{equation}
The previous finite criterion was
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\det U\neq0.
}
\label{eq:detU_previous}
\end{equation}

\subsection{2. Multiplicity-one irrep hypothesis}

Assume now:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(\mathcal E_W\) is a multiplicity-one \(2\)-dimensional isotypic component of the low-energy bundle;
\item the protected left-handed target sector carrying the would-be weak doublet is also a multiplicity-one copy of the same \(2\)-dimensional irreducible representation.
\end{enumerate}

This is exactly the natural finite weak-bundle check already isolated in the notes. :contentReference[oaicite:3]{index=3}  
It is also consistent with the general reduction that a relevant \(2\)-dimensional irrep, once specified, forces a \(2\)-dimensional low-energy sector. :contentReference[oaicite:4]{index=4}

\subsection{3. Schur reduction}

Under the above hypothesis, the overlap map
\[
U:\mathcal E_W\to Z_{\mathrm{top}}^{(2)}
\]
is an intertwiner between two copies of the same irreducible \(2\)-dimensional module.

By Schur's lemma, any such intertwiner is a scalar:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
U=s\,\mathbf 1_2
}
\label{eq:U_scalar_identity}
\end{equation}
for some complex number \(s\).

Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\det U=s^2.
}
\label{eq:detU_s2}
\end{equation}

Therefore the full-rank weak-chirality test reduces to
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\det U\neq0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
s\neq0.
}
\label{eq:s_nonzero_equiv}
\end{equation}

\subsection{4. Practical meaning}

So, under the multiplicity-one irrep hypothesis, one no longer needs all four overlaps \(U_{mA}\).
It is enough to compute one diagonal overlap, for example
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
s=\langle u_1,\Psi_1\rangle
}
\label{eq:s_one_overlap}
\end{equation}
in a symmetry-adapted basis.

If \(s\neq0\), then automatically
\[
U=s\mathbf 1_2,
\qquad
\det U\neq0,
\]
and the weak bundle survives with full rank inside the protected left-handed sector.

\subsection{5. Honest status}

This reduction is conditional on the multiplicity-one identification of both sectors.
That identification is exactly the kind of finite microscopic bundle check that still remains open in the electroweak program. The notes explicitly state that the final identification of these \(Q\)-valued modes with actual Standard-Model chiral electroweak doublets is not yet proved. :contentReference[oaicite:5]{index=5}

So the sharpened next test is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{if the weak bundle and the protected left sector are the same multiplicity-one \(2\)-irrep, compute one scalar }s.
}
\label{eq:sharpened_next_test}
\end{equation}

Then
\[
s\neq0
\]
already proves microscopic \(SU(2)_L\) chirality selection.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{The True Core Open for \(SU(2)_L\): Realization of the Same Isolated Multiplicity-One \(2\)-Irrep}

\subsection{Short Korean explanation}

The reduction
\[
U=s\,\mathbf 1_2
\]
is valid only after one proves that the weak bundle and the relevant protected left-handed sector are the same multiplicity-one isolated \(2\)-dimensional irrep.

So the real next electroweak step is not yet the scalar overlap \(s\neq0\), but the irrep-realization and spectral-isolation check.

\subsection{1. What rank-two geometry does and does not prove}

The already-proved geometric fact
\[
Q=(\mathbb Cz)^\perp
\]
has complex rank \(2\), so each fiber is locally a \(2\)-dimensional \(U(2)\)-module.

However, this does not imply that the actual low-energy eigenspace has dimension \(2\):
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathrm{rank}_{\mathbb C}(Q)=2
\;\not\Rightarrow\;
\dim \mathcal K = 2.
}
\end{equation}
This is because \(\dim\mathcal K\) is a spectral multiplicity question, not a fiber-rank question. :contentReference[oaicite:4]{index=4}

\subsection{2. What symmetry can prove}

If a relevant little group \(G_{k_*}\) or defect stabilizer acts on the low-energy sector, and if the corresponding sector transforms irreducibly in a \(2\)-dimensional representation, then:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{same isolated \(2\)-dimensional irrep}
\Longrightarrow
\dim\mathcal K=2.
}
\end{equation}
This is the exact two-dimensional irrep forcing theorem. 

\subsection{3. What symmetry cannot prove by itself}

Even after identifying a \(2\)-dimensional irrep, symmetry alone does not prove:
\begin{enumerate}
\item that this irrep is the actual low-energy sector,
\item that it is spectrally isolated,
\item that it carries the nondegenerate physical crossing.
\end{enumerate}
So the honest residual assumptions remain:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{specific \(2\)-dimensional irrep is realized},
\quad
\text{it is spectrally isolated}.
}
\end{equation}
This is explicitly stated in the notes. 

\subsection{4. Weak-bundle version of the same problem}

The Standard-Model verification checklist formulates the weak-bundle task as:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Construct }
\mathcal E_W\subset\mathcal E_0
\text{ as a multiplicity-one \(2\)-dimensional isotypic component.}
}
\end{equation}
So the microscopic weak problem is already reduced to an isotypic-component realization problem. :contentReference[oaicite:7]{index=7}

\subsection{5. Once this is proved, Schur reduction follows}

Suppose one has proved that:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(\mathcal E_W\) is a multiplicity-one isolated \(2\)-dimensional irrep;
\item the relevant protected left-handed target sector \(Z_{\mathrm{top}}^{(2)}\) is another multiplicity-one copy of the same irrep.
\end{enumerate}

Then the overlap map
\[
U:\mathcal E_W\to Z_{\mathrm{top}}^{(2)}
\]
is an intertwiner between two copies of the same irreducible module, so by Schur's lemma
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
U=s\,\mathbf 1_2
}
\end{equation}
for some \(s\in\mathbb C\), and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\det U=s^2.
}
\end{equation}
Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\det U\neq0
\Longleftrightarrow
s\neq0.
}
\end{equation}

\subsection{6. The actual next finite test}

So the next real electroweak test is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{prove that }
\mathcal E_W
\text{ and }
Z_{\mathrm{top}}^{(2)}
\text{ are the same isolated multiplicity-one \(2\)-irrep.}
}
\end{equation}

Only after that does the problem reduce to the single complex scalar test
\[
s\neq0.
\]

\section{Final Summary}

The current \(SU(2)_L\) open is not yet the overlap scalar \(s\).
The real core open is the realization/isolation problem:

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal E_W
\text{ is a multiplicity-one isolated \(2\)-dimensional irrep,}
\qquad
Z_{\mathrm{top}}^{(2)}
\text{ is the same one.}
}
\]

Once this is established, the full weak-chirality test collapses to
\[
U=s\,\mathbf 1_2,
\qquad
\det U=s^2,
\]
so it is enough to check one scalar
\[
\boxed{s\neq0.}
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Closure of the “Weak Determinant” Issue: the correct object is the traceless \(3+2\) embedding, not \(\det(\mathcal E_W)\) alone}

\subsection{Short Korean explanation}

If the canonical weak candidate is
\[
\mathcal E_W=T^{1,0}\mathbb{CP}^2,
\]
then its determinant line is not trivial.
So the naive standalone test
\[
\det(\mathcal E_W)\cong \mathcal O
\]
cannot be the correct final weak reduction criterion.

The correct object is the total rank-five bundle
\[
V=E_3\oplus E_W,
\]
whose determinant is trivial and whose structure group reduces to
\[
S(U(3)\times U(2)).
\]

\subsection{1. Canonical \(3+2\) bundle data}

The canonical rank-two and rank-three pieces are
\begin{equation}
E_2:=T^{1,0}\mathbb{CP}^2,
\qquad
E_3:=L\otimes \underline{\mathbb C}^3.
\end{equation}
Their first Chern classes are
\begin{equation}
c_1(E_2)=3h,
\qquad
c_1(E_3)=-3h.
\end{equation}
Hence
\begin{equation}
V:=E_3\oplus E_2
\end{equation}
satisfies
\begin{equation}
c_1(V)=c_1(E_3)+c_1(E_2)=0,
\qquad
\det V\cong \mathcal O.
\end{equation}
Therefore the structure group reduces to
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
S(U(3)\times U(2)).
}
\end{equation}
This part is already closed. 

\subsection{2. Why the bare weak determinant test fails literally}

If one identifies
\[
\mathcal E_W=E_2=T^{1,0}\mathbb{CP}^2,
\]
then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
c_1(\mathcal E_W)=3h\neq 0.
}
\end{equation}
So the bare determinant line \(\det(\mathcal E_W)\) is not topologically trivial.

Hence the standalone sufficient condition
\[
\det(\mathcal E_W)\cong\mathcal O
\]
cannot hold literally for the canonical weak candidate.

So the weak \(SU(2)\) reduction lemma is not the final statement for the bare bundle \(\mathcal E_W\) itself; it is only a sufficient local reduction criterion in an abstract rank-two setting. :contentReference[oaicite:4]{index=4}

\subsection{3. Correct electroweak splitting inside \(S(U(3)\times U(2))\)}

Because \(\det V\cong\mathcal O\), the trace parts of the color and weak blocks are not independent.
Write the total unitary connection on \(V=E_3\oplus E_2\) as
\begin{equation}
\mathcal A_V=
\begin{pmatrix}
\mathcal A_C & 0\\
0 & \mathcal A_W
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\operatorname{tr}\mathcal A_C+\operatorname{tr}\mathcal A_W=0.
\end{equation}
Then one may decompose
\begin{equation}
\mathcal A_C=G_\mu-\frac13 B_\mu\,\mathbf 1_3,
\qquad
\mathcal A_W=W_\mu+\frac12 B_\mu\,\mathbf 1_2,
\end{equation}
with
\[
G_\mu\in\mathfrak{su}(3),\qquad
W_\mu\in\mathfrak{su}(2).
\]
Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal A_V
=
G_\mu\oplus W_\mu
+
B_\mu\,Y_0,
}
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
Y_0=\mathrm{diag}\!\left(
-\frac13,-\frac13,-\frac13,\frac12,\frac12
\right).
}
\end{equation}
This is exactly the primitive traceless central \(U(1)\) direction on the \(3+2\) splitting. 

\subsection{4. What is actually closed}

Therefore the following is already closed, modulo the \(3+2\) identification:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the electroweak/color trace data organize naturally into }
S(U(3)\times U(2)),
\text{ not into a bare }SU(2)\text{ bundle alone.}
}
\end{equation}

So the correct weak-side statement is not
\[
\det(\mathcal E_W)\ \text{trivial by itself},
\]
but rather
\[
\boxed{
\det(E_3\oplus E_W)\cong\mathcal O
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\text{one shared traceless }U(1)\text{ plus weak traceless }SU(2).
}
\]

\subsection{5. The next real open}

Hence the real next open is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{prove that the actual low-energy matter bundle realizes the canonical }3+2\text{ splitting}
}
\end{equation}
together with
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the physical Abelian generator is precisely the primitive traceless }Y_0.
}
\end{equation}
This is exactly the content of the canonical \(3+2\) splitting and primitive hypercharge lemmas, which remain as finite microscopic checks. 

\section{Final Summary}

For the canonical weak candidate
\[
\mathcal E_W=T^{1,0}\mathbb{CP}^2,
\]
the bare weak determinant is not trivial:
\[
c_1(\mathcal E_W)=3h\neq 0.
\]
So the standalone condition
\[
\det(\mathcal E_W)\cong\mathcal O
\]
is not the correct final electroweak criterion.

The correct closed geometric statement is instead:
\[
E_3=L\otimes \underline{\mathbb C}^3,\qquad
E_W=T^{1,0}\mathbb{CP}^2,\qquad
c_1(E_3)+c_1(E_W)=0,
\]
hence
\[
\det(E_3\oplus E_W)\cong\mathcal O,
\qquad
\text{structure group }S(U(3)\times U(2)).
\]

Inside this traceless \(3+2\) structure, the total connection decomposes as
\[
\mathcal A_V
=
G_\mu\oplus W_\mu
+
B_\mu\,\mathrm{diag}\!\left(-\frac13,-\frac13,-\frac13,\frac12,\frac12\right),
\]
so the weak traceless part is \(SU(2)\), while the trace part is absorbed into the unique central hypercharge direction.

Therefore the next real Standard-Model open is not bare weak determinant triviality, but:
\[
\boxed{
\text{prove the actual canonical }3+2\text{ splitting and the primitive hypercharge generator }Y_0.
}
\]

\section{The Next Actual Open: Finite Test for Primitive Hypercharge Embedding}

\subsection{Short Korean explanation}

Under the spectral-isolation hypothesis, the natural low-energy rank-five bundle is already
\[
V=E_3\oplus E_2
=
(L\otimes \underline{\mathbb C}^3)\oplus T^{1,0}\mathbb{CP}^2,
\]
with
\[
\operatorname{rank}(E_3)=3,\qquad \operatorname{rank}(E_2)=2,\qquad c_1(V)=0.
\]
So the true next open is no longer the existence of a \(3+2\) decomposition in principle, but whether the physical Abelian generator is exactly the primitive traceless one on that splitting.

\subsection{1. Canonical \(3+2\) bundle data}

The geometric bundle data are
\begin{equation}
E_3:=L\otimes \underline{\mathbb C}^3,
\qquad
E_2:=T^{1,0}\mathbb{CP}^2,
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
c_1(E_3)=-3h,
\qquad
c_1(E_2)=3h.
\end{equation}
Hence
\begin{equation}
V:=E_3\oplus E_2
\end{equation}
satisfies
\begin{equation}
c_1(V)=0,
\qquad
\det V\cong \mathcal O,
\end{equation}
so the structure group reduces to
\begin{equation}
S(U(3)\times U(2)).
\end{equation}
This is already the canonical geometric precursor of the \(3+2\) Standard-Model splitting. 

\subsection{2. Primitive traceless Abelian generator}

On a \(3+2\) splitting, let the Abelian generator act diagonally as
\begin{equation}
Y=\mathrm{diag}(y_C,y_C,y_C,y_W,y_W).
\end{equation}
Tracelessness requires
\begin{equation}
3y_C+2y_W=0.
\label{eq:traceless_constraint}
\end{equation}
The primitive integer solution is uniquely
\begin{equation}
(y_C,y_W)=(-2,3)
\end{equation}
up to overall sign, hence the canonically normalized generator is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
Y_0
=
\mathrm{diag}\!\left(
-\frac13,-\frac13,-\frac13,\frac12,\frac12
\right).
}
\label{eq:Y0_primitive}
\end{equation}
This is the primitive hypercharge lemma. :contentReference[oaicite:5]{index=5}

\subsection{3. What is already proved and what is still open}

What is already proved:
\begin{enumerate}
\item once a canonical \(3+2\) traceless embedding exists, the exact Standard-Model fractions follow from \(Y_0\);
\item hypercharge quantization is rational whenever the underlying projective and determinant charges are integral.
\end{enumerate}

What is still open:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{prove that the actual TECT low-energy matter bundle carries the canonical \(3+2\) splitting}
}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{prove that the physical Abelian generator is exactly }Y_0,
\text{ not another rational combination.}
}
\end{equation}
This is explicitly identified as the remaining hypercharge frontier. 

\subsection{4. Finite test for actual \(Y_0\)-embedding}

Let \(Y_{\mathrm{phys}}\) be the Abelian generator induced on the actual low-energy matter bundle.
Choose one color basis vector \(c\in E_3\) and one weak basis vector \(w\in E_2\), normalized in the actual low-energy bundle.

Define
\begin{equation}
y_C:=\langle c,\,Y_{\mathrm{phys}}\,c\rangle,
\qquad
y_W:=\langle w,\,Y_{\mathrm{phys}}\,w\rangle.
\label{eq:yC_yW_def}
\end{equation}

Then the primitive hypercharge embedding is equivalent to the two conditions
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
3y_C+2y_W=0,
}
\label{eq:finite_test_1}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(y_C,y_W)\propto \left(-\frac13,\frac12\right)
\quad\text{(equivalently, after clearing denominators, }(y_C,y_W)\propto(-2,3)\text{)}.
}
\label{eq:finite_test_2}
\end{equation}

Because the traceless primitive ratio is unique up to sign, this is already enough to force
\[
Y_{\mathrm{phys}}=\pm Y_0
\]
on the \(3+2\) decomposition.

\subsection{5. Consequence}

Once \eqref{eq:finite_test_1}--\eqref{eq:finite_test_2} are verified, the tensor-sector hypercharges follow additively:
\[
-\frac13+\frac12=\frac16,
\qquad
-\frac13-\frac13=-\frac23,
\qquad
\frac12+\frac12=1,
\]
and therefore the exact Standard-Model fractions are closed at the theorem level. :contentReference[oaicite:7]{index=7}

\section{Final Summary}

The next actual Standard-Model open is not the abstract existence of electroweak geometry.
It is the finite primitive hypercharge test.

Under the canonical \(3+2\) bundle data
\[
V=E_3\oplus E_2,
\qquad
E_3=L\otimes \underline{\mathbb C}^3,
\qquad
E_2=T^{1,0}\mathbb{CP}^2,
\]
the physical Abelian generator must be tested against the primitive traceless form
\[
Y_0=\mathrm{diag}\!\left(-\frac13,-\frac13,-\frac13,\frac12,\frac12\right).
\]

So the next finite check is:
\[
\boxed{
y_C=\langle c,Y_{\mathrm{phys}}c\rangle,
\qquad
y_W=\langle w,Y_{\mathrm{phys}}w\rangle,
}
\]
followed by
\[
\boxed{
3y_C+2y_W=0,
\qquad
(y_C,y_W)\propto\left(-\frac13,\frac12\right).
}
\]

If this holds, then the primitive hypercharge embedding is closed and the exact Standard-Model charge fractions follow.

\section{Microscopic Reduction of Family Hierarchy and Mixing to a Finite \(3\times 3\) Operator Problem}

\subsection{Short Korean explanation}

Once the common family space is fixed as
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}=H^0(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathcal O(1))\cong\mathbb C^3,
\]
the hierarchy and mixing problem is no longer a vague generational question.
It is the concrete problem of determining, for each fermion sector
\[
f\in\{u,d,e,\nu\},
\]
the effective Hermitian operator \(H_f\) on the same three-dimensional family space.

\subsection{1. Common family Hilbert space}

At minimal positive level,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}=H^0(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathcal O(1))\cong\mathbb C^3.
}
\label{eq:Hfam_common}
\end{equation}
Thus all fermion sectors live on the same three-dimensional family Hilbert space. :contentReference[oaicite:3]{index=3}

\subsection{2. Sector-dependent effective operators}

For each fermion sector
\[
f\in\{u,d,e,\nu\},
\]
assume the effective family operator has the form
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_f = m_{0,f}\mathbf 1_3 + \lambda_f T^{(1)}(V_f)+\Delta_f^{\mathrm{np}},
}
\label{eq:Hf_general}
\end{equation}
where:
\begin{itemize}
\item \(m_{0,f}\mathbf 1_3\) is the universal sectoral offset,
\item \(T^{(1)}(V_f)\) is the leading anisotropy/overlap lifting operator,
\item \(\Delta_f^{\mathrm{np}}\) denotes subleading nonperturbative or tunneling corrections.
\end{itemize}
This is exactly the structural reduction stated in the notes. :contentReference[oaicite:4]{index=4}

\subsection{3. Mass matrices in a localized family basis}

Choose a semiclassical localized basis
\[
\{\phi_1,\phi_2,\phi_3\}
\]
of \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}\), each concentrated near one anisotropy-selected region of the defect moduli space.

Then the effective mass matrix of sector \(f\) is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(M_f)_{ij}=\langle \phi_i,\mathcal Y_f \phi_j\rangle,
}
\label{eq:Mf_def}
\end{equation}
where \(\mathcal Y_f\) is the effective internal Yukawa/overlap operator for that sector. :contentReference[oaicite:5]{index=5}

In the adiabatic/semiclassical regime, the diagonal entries are exponentially split,
\begin{equation}
(M_f)_{ii}\sim A_{f,i}e^{-S_{f,i}},
\label{eq:diag_hierarchy}
\end{equation}
while the off-diagonal entries are overlap/tunneling amplitudes
\begin{equation}
(M_f)_{ij}\sim \epsilon_{ij}^{(f)},\qquad i\neq j.
\label{eq:offdiag_overlap}
\end{equation}
So the canonical finite form is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
M_f\approx
\begin{pmatrix}
A_{f,1}e^{-S_{f,1}} & \epsilon_{12}^{(f)} & \epsilon_{13}^{(f)}\\
\epsilon_{12}^{(f)} & A_{f,2}e^{-S_{f,2}} & \epsilon_{23}^{(f)}\\
\epsilon_{13}^{(f)} & \epsilon_{23}^{(f)} & A_{f,3}e^{-S_{f,3}}
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\label{eq:Mf_canonical}
\end{equation}
:contentReference[oaicite:6]{index=6}

\subsection{4. Diagonalization and mixing matrices}

Let
\begin{equation}
U_f^\dagger H_f U_f = \mathrm{diag}(m_{f1},m_{f2},m_{f3}).
\label{eq:Hf_diag}
\end{equation}
Then the physical quark and lepton mixing matrices are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V_{\mathrm{CKM}} = U_u^\dagger U_d,
}
\label{eq:CKM_formula}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
U_{\mathrm{PMNS}} = U_e^\dagger U_\nu.
}
\label{eq:PMNS_formula}
\end{equation}
This is already the structural theorem in the notes. 

\subsection{5. Perturbative interpretation}

If off-diagonal overlaps are small relative to level splittings, then for \(i\neq j\)
\begin{equation}
\theta^{(f)}_{ij}
\approx
\frac{\epsilon_{ij}^{(f)}}{m_{fj}^{(0)}-m_{fi}^{(0)}},
\qquad
m_{fi}^{(0)}:=A_{f,i}e^{-S_{f,i}}.
\label{eq:pert_theta}
\end{equation}
Therefore:
\begin{itemize}
\item small \(\epsilon_{ij}^{(u,d)}\) and/or near alignment of \(H_u,H_d\) imply
\[
V_{\mathrm{CKM}}\approx \mathbf 1;
\]
\item stronger misalignment between \(H_e\) and \(H_\nu\) naturally allows large PMNS angles.
\end{itemize}
This matches the qualitative theorem stated in the notes. :contentReference[oaicite:8]{index=8}

\subsection{6. What is already closed and what remains open}

What is already closed:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{hierarchy and mixing reduce to sector-dependent operators on one common } \mathbb C^3 \text{ family space.}
}
\label{eq:closed_hierarchy}
\end{equation}

What remains open:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{derive the actual microscopic operators }V_f,\ \Delta_f^{\mathrm{np}},\ \mathcal Y_f
\text{ from the TECT defect PDE.}
}
\label{eq:open_hierarchy}
\end{equation}
In particular, the notes explicitly leave open:
\begin{enumerate}
\item the exact microscopic sectoral operators \(V_f\) and \(\Delta_f^{\mathrm{np}}\),
\item the observed numerical CKM/PMNS values,
\item the Dirac/Majorana nature of the neutrino sector.
\end{enumerate}


\section{Final Summary}

The next real Standard-Model frontier in TECT is not generation counting anymore.
It is the finite \(3\times3\) family-operator problem.

All fermion sectors act on the same family Hilbert space
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}=H^0(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathcal O(1))\cong\mathbb C^3.
\]
Each sector is governed by an operator
\[
H_f = m_{0,f}\mathbf 1_3 + \lambda_f T^{(1)}(V_f)+\Delta_f^{\mathrm{np}},
\]
or equivalently by a finite mass matrix
\[
(M_f)_{ij}=\langle \phi_i,\mathcal Y_f\phi_j\rangle.
\]

Then
\[
V_{\mathrm{CKM}}=U_u^\dagger U_d,
\qquad
U_{\mathrm{PMNS}}=U_e^\dagger U_\nu,
\]
and hierarchy/mixing come entirely from the relative misalignment of the sector-dependent operators on the same three-dimensional family space.

So the next nonredundant calculation is:
\[
\boxed{
\text{derive the actual sectoral overlap/tunneling operators } \mathcal Y_f \text{ from the full TECT defect PDE.}
}
\]

+

U_f^\dagger H_f U_f

\boxed{
V_{\mathrm{CKM}}=U_u^\dagger U_d,
\qquad
U_{\mathrm{PMNS}}=U_e^\dagger U_\nu.
}
\label{eq:mixing_final}
\end{equation}

\section{5. What is actually still open}

It is the microscopic derivation of the operator \(\mathcal Y_f\) itself from the TECT defect PDE.

The minimal exact next target is therefore:

\label{eq:next_target_Yf}

\langle \phi_i,\mathcal Y_f\phi_j\rangle;

\section{Actual extraction of the sectoral family operator from the full defect PDE}

\subsection{Setup}

Let the full microscopic defect Hamiltonian (or first-order Dirac-type operator after reduction)
be written as
\begin{equation}
H_f^{\mathrm{micro}} = H_0 + \delta H_f,
\label{eq:Hmicro_split}
\end{equation}
where:
\begin{itemize}
\item \(H_0\) is the family-universal leading operator,
\item \(\delta H_f\) is the sector-dependent microscopic perturbation distinguishing
\(f\in\{u,d,e,\nu\}\).
\end{itemize}

Assume that \(H_0\) has a three-dimensional protected low-energy family subspace
\begin{equation}
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}} = \mathrm{span}\{\phi_1,\phi_2,\phi_3\},
\qquad
H_0\phi_i = E_* \phi_i,
\label{eq:family_subspace}
\end{equation}
with orthogonal projector
\begin{equation}
P = \sum_{i=1}^3 |\phi_i\rangle\langle \phi_i|,
\qquad
Q = 1-P.
\label{eq:PQ_def}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Exact Feshbach/Schur-complement family operator}

The exact energy-dependent effective operator on the family space is
\begin{equation}
H_f^{\mathrm{eff}}(E)
=
PHP
+
PHQ\,(E-QH_f^{\mathrm{micro}}Q)^{-1}\,QHP,
\label{eq:Feshbach_exact}
\end{equation}
where \(H_f^{\mathrm{micro}}=H_0+\delta H_f\).

Expanding around the unperturbed family energy \(E_*\), one gets the standard degenerate effective operator
\begin{equation}
\mathcal Y_f
=
P\,\delta H_f\,P
-
P\,\delta H_f\,Q\,(QH_0Q-E_*)^{-1}\,Q\,\delta H_f\,P
+
O(\delta H_f^3).
\label{eq:Yf_SW}
\end{equation}

This is the actual sectoral family operator.

\subsection{Finite \(3\times 3\) family matrix}

In the family basis \(\{\phi_i\}\),
\begin{equation}
(M_f)_{ij}
=
\langle \phi_i,\mathcal Y_f\phi_j\rangle.
\label{eq:Mf_from_Yf}
\end{equation}
Using \eqref{eq:Yf_SW},
\begin{align}
(M_f)_{ij}
&=
\langle \phi_i,\delta H_f\phi_j\rangle
-
\sum_{n\in Q}
\frac{
\langle \phi_i,\delta H_f n\rangle
\langle n,\delta H_f\phi_j\rangle
}{
E_n-E_*
}
+
O(\delta H_f^3),
\label{eq:Mf_explicit}
\end{align}
where \(QH_0Q\,|n\rangle = E_n |n\rangle\).

Thus every sector reduces to a finite \(3\times3\) matrix problem.

\subsection{Localized family basis and semiclassical form}

Choose a localized basis \(\phi_i\) on the three family centers/wells of the defect moduli space.
Then the matrix naturally separates into diagonal lifting terms and off-diagonal overlap/tunneling terms:
\begin{equation}
(M_f)_{ii}
=
m_{0,f}
+
\Delta_{f,i}^{\mathrm{loc}}
+
\Delta_{f,i}^{\mathrm{virt}}
+\cdots,
\label{eq:diag_entries}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
(M_f)_{ij}
=
T_{ij}^{(f)}
+
V_{ij}^{(f)}
+\cdots,
\qquad
i\neq j,
\label{eq:offdiag_entries}
\end{equation}
where
\begin{itemize}
\item \(\Delta_{f,i}^{\mathrm{loc}}=\langle \phi_i,\delta H_f\phi_i\rangle\) is the local diagonal lifting,
\item \(\Delta_{f,i}^{\mathrm{virt}}\) is the second-order virtual correction through \(Q\),
\item \(T_{ij}^{(f)}=\langle \phi_i,\delta H_f\phi_j\rangle\) is the direct overlap/tunneling amplitude,
\item \(V_{ij}^{(f)}\) is the indirect second-order mixing through remote states.
\end{itemize}

In semiclassical form this becomes
\begin{equation}
M_f
\approx
\begin{pmatrix}
A_{f,1}e^{-S_{f,1}} & \epsilon_{12}^{(f)} & \epsilon_{13}^{(f)}\\
\epsilon_{12}^{(f)} & A_{f,2}e^{-S_{f,2}} & \epsilon_{23}^{(f)}\\
\epsilon_{13}^{(f)} & \epsilon_{23}^{(f)} & A_{f,3}e^{-S_{f,3}}
\end{pmatrix},
\label{eq:Mf_semiclassical}
\end{equation}
with
\[
\epsilon_{ij}^{(f)}
=
T_{ij}^{(f)}+V_{ij}^{(f)}+\cdots .
\]

\subsection{Microscopic integral form}

If the full defect PDE is written in position space and the family wavefunctions are \(\phi_i(x)\),
then the first-order term is
\begin{equation}
\langle \phi_i,\delta H_f\phi_j\rangle
=
\int d^3x\,
\phi_i^\dagger(x)\,
\delta h_f(x)\,
\phi_j(x),
\label{eq:first_order_integral}
\end{equation}
where \(\delta h_f(x)\) is the local sector-distinguishing kernel extracted from the defect background.

The second-order term is
\begin{equation}
-\sum_{n\in Q}
\frac{
\left(\int d^3x\,\phi_i^\dagger(x)\delta h_f(x)n(x)\right)
\left(\int d^3y\,n^\dagger(y)\delta h_f(y)\phi_j(y)\right)
}{
E_n-E_*
}.
\label{eq:second_order_integral}
\end{equation}

Hence the only genuinely missing microscopic input is the explicit kernel \(\delta h_f(x)\) for each sector.

\subsection{Diagonalization and physical observables}

For each sector,
\begin{equation}
U_f^\dagger M_f U_f
=
\mathrm{diag}(m_{f1},m_{f2},m_{f3}).
\label{eq:diag_Mf}
\end{equation}
Then
\begin{equation}
V_{\mathrm{CKM}} = U_u^\dagger U_d,
\qquad
U_{\mathrm{PMNS}} = U_e^\dagger U_\nu.
\label{eq:CKM_PMNS_again}
\end{equation}

So the entire flavor problem is reduced to computing four finite \(3\times3\) matrices:
\[
M_u,\quad M_d,\quad M_e,\quad M_\nu.
\]

\subsection{What remains open}

The remaining open problem is not the matrix algebra.
It is the derivation of the sector-dependent microscopic perturbations
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta H_u,\qquad
\delta H_d,\qquad
\delta H_e,\qquad
\delta H_\nu
}
\label{eq:deltaHf_open}
\end{equation}
from the full TECT defect PDE.

Once these are known, the family matrices follow from
\eqref{eq:Mf_explicit}, and all hierarchy/mixing observables are determined by finite diagonalization.

\section{Final summary}

The remaining Standard-Model frontier is the extraction of the four sectoral family operators from the full defect PDE.

Let
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}=\mathrm{span}\{\phi_1,\phi_2,\phi_3\},
\qquad
P=\sum_{i=1}^3 |\phi_i\rangle\langle\phi_i|,
\qquad
Q=1-P.
\]
For each sector \(f\in\{u,d,e,\nu\}\),
\[
H_f^{\mathrm{micro}}=H_0+\delta H_f,
\]
and the effective family operator is
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_f
=
P\,\delta H_f\,P
-
P\,\delta H_f\,Q\,(QH_0Q-E_*)^{-1}\,Q\,\delta H_f\,P
+
O(\delta H_f^3).
}
\]

Thus the finite family matrix is
\[
\boxed{
(M_f)_{ij}
=
\langle \phi_i,\mathcal Y_f\phi_j\rangle.
}
\]

Once \(M_u,M_d,M_e,M_\nu\) are computed, one diagonalizes:
\[
U_f^\dagger M_f U_f=\mathrm{diag}(m_{f1},m_{f2},m_{f3}),
\]
and obtains
\[
V_{\mathrm{CKM}}=U_u^\dagger U_d,
\qquad
U_{\mathrm{PMNS}}=U_e^\dagger U_\nu.
\]

Therefore the only genuine remaining microscopic task is:
\[
\boxed{
\text{derive } \delta H_u,\ \delta H_d,\ \delta H_e,\ \delta H_\nu
\text{ from the full TECT defect PDE.}
}
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Actual next reduction: five chiral family operators before the neutrino branch}

\subsection{Short Korean explanation}

The next microscopic flavor problem should not be formulated first in terms of
\[
u,d,e,\nu,
\]
because before electroweak symmetry breaking and before deciding the neutrino branch,
the canonical one-family bundle is organized into the five chiral Standard-Model blocks
\[
Q,\ u,\ d,\ L,\ e.
\]

Thus the first actual family-operator extraction problem is to compute
\[
H_Q,\ H_u,\ H_d,\ H_L,\ H_e
\]
on the common family Hilbert space.

\subsection{1. Canonical one-family decomposition}

The canonical chiral family bundle is
\begin{equation}
\mathcal F_a
=
(\mathbf 3,\mathbf 2)_a
\oplus
(\mathbf 1,\mathbf 2)_{-3a}
\oplus
(\overline{\mathbf 3},\mathbf 1)_{-4a}
\oplus
(\overline{\mathbf 3},\mathbf 1)_{2a}
\oplus
(\mathbf 1,\mathbf 1)_{6a}.
\label{eq:Fa_blocks}
\end{equation}
For the Standard-Model normalization
\[
a=\frac16,
\]
this is exactly
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal F_{\mathrm{SM}}
=
Q\oplus L\oplus u^c\oplus d^c\oplus e^c.
}
\label{eq:FSM_blocks}
\end{equation}
So the natural pre-EWSB matter blocks are the five chiral sectors
\[
Q,\ u,\ d,\ L,\ e.
\]


\subsection{2. Common family factor}

All these sectors carry the same generation Hilbert space
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}=H^0(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathcal O(1))\cong\mathbb C^3.
}
\label{eq:Hfam_again}
\end{equation}
Hence the protected chiral matter space is of the form
\begin{equation}
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{chiral}}
=
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}\otimes \mathcal F_{\mathrm{SM}}.
\label{eq:Hchiral_factor}
\end{equation}

\subsection{3. Gauge-equivariant operator decomposition}

Let \(\mathcal Y\) be any gauge-equivariant microscopic family operator acting on
\(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{chiral}}\).
Because different Standard-Model blocks in \eqref{eq:FSM_blocks} carry inequivalent
\[
SU(3)_c\times SU(2)_L\times U(1)_Y
\]
quantum numbers, gauge equivariance forbids inter-block mixing between distinct irreducible sectors.

Therefore, by block decomposition and Schur-type reduction,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal Y
=
\bigoplus_{r\in\{Q,u,d,L,e\}}
\mathbf 1_{R_r}\otimes H_r,
}
\label{eq:Y_block_diag}
\end{equation}
where \(R_r\) is the corresponding gauge-representation block and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_r:\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}\to\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}
}
\label{eq:Hr_def}
\end{equation}
is a Hermitian \(3\times3\) family operator.

Thus the actual next finite extraction problem is not one operator but five:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_Q,\qquad H_u,\qquad H_d,\qquad H_L,\qquad H_e.
}
\label{eq:five_Hr}
\end{equation}

\subsection{4. Matrix extraction in a family basis}

Choose a family basis
\[
\phi_1,\phi_2,\phi_3\in\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}.
\]
Then for each chiral block \(r\in\{Q,u,d,L,e\}\),
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(M_r)_{ij}
=
\langle \phi_i,H_r\phi_j\rangle.
}
\label{eq:Mr_extract}
\end{equation}
These are the five finite \(3\times3\) matrices that the full defect PDE must produce first.

\subsection{5. Why neutrino is not first}

In the minimal family geometry, the left-handed neutrino sits automatically inside
\[
L=(\mathbf1,\mathbf2)_{-1/2},
\]
but an independent sterile singlet
\[
N\sim(\mathbf1,\mathbf1)_0
\]
is not forced.
Therefore the neutrino sector is still branch-dependent:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Dirac branch: add }N\text{ and compute }H_\nu^{\mathrm{Dirac}};
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Majorana branch: add a }\Delta L=2\text{ pairing channel and compute }H_\nu^{\mathrm{Maj}}.
}
\end{equation}
So \(H_\nu\) is not the first family operator to derive microscopically.
It comes only after the sterile/pairing question is decided.
:contentReference[oaicite:4]{index=4}

\subsection{6. Actual next microscopic target}

Therefore the next real microscopic flavor target is:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{derive the five block operators }H_Q,H_u,H_d,H_L,H_e
\text{ from the full TECT defect PDE.}
}
\label{eq:actual_next_target_five}
\end{equation}
Only after these are known should one proceed to:
\begin{enumerate}
\item EWSB splitting inside the weak doublet \(L\) and \(Q\),
\item the neutrino branch (Dirac vs Majorana),
\item CKM/PMNS extraction from the post-EWSB sector matrices.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final Summary}

The next flavor problem is not yet the four post-EWSB sector matrices
\[
u,d,e,\nu.
\]
The correct pre-EWSB microscopic problem is the extraction of the five chiral family operators
\[
\boxed{
H_Q,\ H_u,\ H_d,\ H_L,\ H_e.
}
\]

They arise because the canonical one-family bundle is
\[
\mathcal F_{\mathrm{SM}}=Q\oplus L\oplus u^c\oplus d^c\oplus e^c,
\]
and gauge equivariance forces the microscopic family operator to be block diagonal:
\[
\mathcal Y
=
\bigoplus_{r\in\{Q,u,d,L,e\}}
\mathbf1_{R_r}\otimes H_r.
\]

So the genuinely next nonredundant calculation is:
\[
\boxed{
(M_r)_{ij}=\langle \phi_i,H_r\phi_j\rangle
\qquad
(r=Q,u,d,L,e).
}
\]

The neutrino family operator is secondary and remains branch-dependent until the sterile-singlet / \(\Delta L=2\) problem is resolved.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Pre-EWSB block decomposition of the microscopic flavor operator}

\subsection{Short Korean explanation}

Before electroweak symmetry breaking, the microscopic family operator must respect the unbroken gauge representation labels.
Therefore it cannot mix inequivalent Standard-Model chiral blocks.
This forces the full operator to reduce to five separate \(3\times 3\) family operators:
\[
H_Q,\ H_u,\ H_d,\ H_L,\ H_e.
\]

\section{1. Common family and one-family factorization}

Let
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}} \cong \mathbb C^3
\]
be the common family Hilbert space, and let the one-family chiral matter representation be
\[
\mathcal F_{\mathrm{SM}}=Q\oplus L\oplus u^c\oplus d^c\oplus e^c .
\]
Then the protected pre-EWSB chiral matter space is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{chiral}}
=
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}\otimes \mathcal F_{\mathrm{SM}}.
}
\label{eq:Hchiral_preEWSB}
\end{equation}

\section{2. Gauge-equivariant block theorem}

Let \(\mathcal Y_{\mathrm{micro}}\) be the effective microscopic family operator induced from the full defect PDE on \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{chiral}}\).
Assume \(\mathcal Y_{\mathrm{micro}}\) commutes with the unbroken pre-EWSB gauge action
\[
SU(3)_c\times SU(2)_L\times U(1)_Y.
\]
Because the five chiral blocks
\[
Q,\ L,\ u^c,\ d^c,\ e^c
\]
carry inequivalent irreducible gauge quantum numbers, Schur-type reasoning implies that no off-block map is gauge-equivariant.

Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_{\mathrm{micro}}
=
\mathbf 1_Q\otimes H_Q
\;\oplus\;
\mathbf 1_L\otimes H_L
\;\oplus\;
\mathbf 1_{u^c}\otimes H_u
\;\oplus\;
\mathbf 1_{d^c}\otimes H_d
\;\oplus\;
\mathbf 1_{e^c}\otimes H_e ,
}
\label{eq:block_diag_five}
\end{equation}
where each
\[
H_r:\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}\to\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}},
\qquad
r\in\{Q,u,d,L,e\},
\]
is Hermitian \(3\times3\).

So the next microscopic extraction problem is not one operator but exactly five:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_Q,\qquad H_u,\qquad H_d,\qquad H_L,\qquad H_e.
}
\label{eq:five_operators}
\end{equation}

\section{3. Feshbach extraction formula for each block}

Let the full microscopic operator in the relevant defect background be
\[
\mathbb H_r^{\mathrm{micro}}=\mathbb H_{0,r}+\delta \mathbb H_r
\]
on the full sector-\(r\) Hilbert space, and let
\[
P_r
\]
project onto the three-dimensional protected family subspace of that block, with
\[
Q_r=1-P_r.
\]
Then the exact energy-dependent Feshbach operator is
\begin{equation}
H_r^{\mathrm{eff}}(E)
=
P_r \mathbb H_r^{\mathrm{micro}} P_r
+
P_r \mathbb H_r^{\mathrm{micro}} Q_r
\bigl(E-Q_r\mathbb H_r^{\mathrm{micro}}Q_r\bigr)^{-1}
Q_r \mathbb H_r^{\mathrm{micro}} P_r .
\label{eq:Feshbach_block}
\end{equation}

Expanding around the protected energy \(E_{*,r}\),
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_r
=
P_r\,\delta\mathbb H_r\,P_r
-
P_r\,\delta\mathbb H_r\,Q_r
\bigl(Q_r\mathbb H_{0,r}Q_r-E_{*,r}\bigr)^{-1}
Q_r\,\delta\mathbb H_r\,P_r
+\cdots .
}
\label{eq:Hr_SW}
\end{equation}

This is the actual microscopic family operator for block \(r\).

\section{4. Finite matrix extraction}

Choose a family basis
\[
\phi_1,\phi_2,\phi_3\in\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}.
\]
Then for each block \(r\),
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(M_r)_{ij}
=
\langle \phi_i,H_r\phi_j\rangle,
\qquad
i,j=1,2,3.
}
\label{eq:Mr_matrix}
\end{equation}
Explicitly,
\begin{align}
(M_r)_{ij}
&=
\langle \phi_i,\delta\mathbb H_r\phi_j\rangle
-
\sum_{n\in Q_r}
\frac{
\langle \phi_i,\delta\mathbb H_r n\rangle
\langle n,\delta\mathbb H_r\phi_j\rangle
}{
E_n-E_{*,r}
}
+\cdots .
\label{eq:Mr_explicit}
\end{align}

So the entire pre-EWSB flavor problem is reduced to five finite \(3\times3\) matrices.

\section{5. Why neutrino is postponed}

At this stage, \(\nu_L\) is already inside
\[
L=(\mathbf1,\mathbf2)_{-1/2},
\]
but an independent sterile singlet
\[
N\sim(\mathbf1,\mathbf1)_0
\]
is not forced by the minimal family geometry.
Therefore neutrino flavor is branch-dependent:
Dirac if a sterile singlet is added, Majorana if a \(\Delta L=2\) pairing channel exists.
So the neutrino matrix is not primary at the pre-EWSB stage. It comes after the sterile/pairing branch is fixed. 

\section{6. Immediate next executable target}

Thus the next nonredundant microscopic target is:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{derive }
\delta\mathbb H_Q,\ \delta\mathbb H_u,\ \delta\mathbb H_d,\ \delta\mathbb H_L,\ \delta\mathbb H_e
\text{ from the full TECT defect PDE,}
}
\end{equation}
and then compute
\[
(M_Q)_{ij},\ (M_u)_{ij},\ (M_d)_{ij},\ (M_L)_{ij},\ (M_e)_{ij}.
\]

Only after this should one pass to:
\begin{enumerate}
\item electroweak/Higgs splitting inside \(Q\) and \(L\),
\item neutrino Dirac vs Majorana branch,
\item CKM/PMNS diagonalization.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final Summary}

The genuine next flavor calculation is the pre-EWSB block extraction theorem:
\[
\mathcal Y_{\mathrm{micro}}
=
\mathbf 1_Q\otimes H_Q
\oplus
\mathbf 1_L\otimes H_L
\oplus
\mathbf 1_{u^c}\otimes H_u
\oplus
\mathbf 1_{d^c}\otimes H_d
\oplus
\mathbf 1_{e^c}\otimes H_e.
\]

Each \(H_r\) is obtained from the full defect PDE by the Feshbach/Schur formula
\[
H_r
=
P_r\,\delta\mathbb H_r\,P_r
-
P_r\,\delta\mathbb H_r\,Q_r
\bigl(Q_r\mathbb H_{0,r}Q_r-E_{*,r}\bigr)^{-1}
Q_r\,\delta\mathbb H_r\,P_r
+\cdots .
\]

Therefore the next real microscopic task is:
\[
\boxed{
\text{compute the five pre-EWSB sector operators }H_Q,H_u,H_d,H_L,H_e.
}
\]

\section{Actual next step: extract one pre-EWSB family operator and split it by hypercharge projectors}

\subsection{Short Korean explanation}

Instead of deriving five unrelated operators
\[
H_Q,\ H_u,\ H_d,\ H_L,\ H_e
\]
one by one, the sharper reduction is to derive a single pre-EWSB microscopic family operator
\[
\mathcal Y_{\rm pre}
\]
on the full chiral family space and then extract the five chiral blocks by spectral projectors of the primitive hypercharge generator \(Y_0\).

\section{1. Common chiral family space}

Let
\[
\mathcal H_{\rm fam}\cong \mathbb C^3
\]
be the common family Hilbert space, and let one Standard-Model family in left-handed notation be
\[
\mathcal F_{\rm SM}
=
Q\oplus L\oplus u^c\oplus d^c\oplus e^c.
\]
Then the protected pre-EWSB chiral matter space is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal H_{\rm chiral}
=
\mathcal H_{\rm fam}\otimes \mathcal F_{\rm SM}.
}
\end{equation}

\section{2. Primitive hypercharge eigenvalues}

Using the canonical normalization \(a=\tfrac16\), the five chiral blocks carry distinct hypercharges
\begin{equation}
y_Q=\frac16,\qquad
y_u=-\frac23,\qquad
y_d=\frac13,\qquad
y_L=-\frac12,\qquad
y_e=1.
\label{eq:five_y_values}
\end{equation}
These five values are pairwise distinct.

Therefore, once the primitive hypercharge generator \(Y_0\) is realized, its spectrum already separates the five pre-EWSB chiral blocks.

\section{3. Hypercharge spectral projectors}

Because the eigenvalues \eqref{eq:five_y_values} are distinct, the projector onto each block can be written as a Lagrange polynomial in \(Y_0\):
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Pi_r(Y_0)
=
\prod_{s\neq r}
\frac{Y_0-y_s\,\mathbf 1}{y_r-y_s},
\qquad
r\in\{Q,u,d,L,e\}.
}
\label{eq:Pi_r_Lagrange}
\end{equation}
These satisfy
\[
\Pi_r\Pi_s=\delta_{rs}\Pi_r,
\qquad
\sum_r \Pi_r=\mathbf 1.
\]

So the five chiral sectors are not an extra assumption anymore; they are the spectral blocks of \(Y_0\).

\section{4. One operator instead of five}

Let the full microscopic defect PDE induce one pre-EWSB gauge-equivariant family operator
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_{\rm pre}
=
P\,\delta\mathbb H_{\rm pre}\,P
-
P\,\delta\mathbb H_{\rm pre}\,Q\,(Q\mathbb H_0Q-E_*)^{-1}\,Q\,\delta\mathbb H_{\rm pre}\,P
+\cdots
}
\label{eq:Y_pre}
\end{equation}
on
\[
\mathcal H_{\rm chiral}=\mathcal H_{\rm fam}\otimes \mathcal F_{\rm SM}.
\]

Assume \(\mathcal Y_{\rm pre}\) commutes with the unbroken pre-EWSB gauge action.
Then the actual block operators are extracted by
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_r
=
\Pi_r(Y_0)\,\mathcal Y_{\rm pre}\,\Pi_r(Y_0)
\Big|_{\mathcal H_{\rm fam}\otimes R_r},
\qquad
r\in\{Q,u,d,L,e\},
}
\label{eq:Hr_from_Ypre}
\end{equation}
where \(R_r\) is the corresponding gauge representation factor.

Thus the five block operators are no longer independent unknowns:
they are all spectral pieces of one operator \(\mathcal Y_{\rm pre}\).

\section{5. Finite \(3\times3\) matrices}

Choose a family basis
\[
\phi_1,\phi_2,\phi_3\in \mathcal H_{\rm fam}.
\]
Then the finite family matrices are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(M_r)_{ij}
=
\langle \phi_i,\ H_r\ \phi_j\rangle
=
\langle \phi_i,\ \Pi_r(Y_0)\,\mathcal Y_{\rm pre}\,\Pi_r(Y_0)\ \phi_j\rangle.
}
\label{eq:Mr_projected}
\end{equation}

So the real microscopic task is now reduced further:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{derive one operator }\mathcal Y_{\rm pre}\text{ from the full defect PDE, then project it with }\Pi_r(Y_0).
}
\label{eq:single_task}
\end{equation}

\section{6. Why this is the correct next step}

This is sharper than introducing five unrelated perturbations
\[
\delta\mathbb H_Q,\ \delta\mathbb H_u,\ \delta\mathbb H_d,\ \delta\mathbb H_L,\ \delta\mathbb H_e,
\]
because gauge-equivariance plus distinct hypercharge eigenvalues already imply that the decomposition is spectral.

So the next genuinely new microscopic calculation is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{compute }\mathcal Y_{\rm pre}\text{ on }\mathcal H_{\rm fam}\otimes \mathcal F_{\rm SM}
\text{ from the full TECT defect PDE.}
}
\end{equation}

After that, the five sectoral family matrices follow automatically from \eqref{eq:Hr_from_Ypre}.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Actual next step: hypercharge-polynomial reduction of the pre-EWSB family operator}

\subsection{Short Korean explanation}

The five pre-EWSB chiral blocks
\[
Q,\ L,\ u^c,\ d^c,\ e^c
\]
have distinct hypercharges.
Therefore any gauge-equivariant pre-EWSB operator can be expanded as a polynomial in the primitive hypercharge generator \(Y_0\), with coefficients acting only on the common family Hilbert space \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}\cong\mathbb C^3\).

This reduces the flavor problem from five unrelated block operators to five family-space coefficient matrices.

\section{1. Common setup}

Let
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}=H^0(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathcal O(1))\cong\mathbb C^3,
\]
and let the pre-EWSB one-family chiral representation be
\[
\mathcal F_{\mathrm{SM}}=Q\oplus L\oplus u^c\oplus d^c\oplus e^c.
\]
The protected chiral matter space is
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{chiral}}
=
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}\otimes \mathcal F_{\mathrm{SM}}.
\]

The primitive hypercharge eigenvalues are
\begin{equation}
y_Q=\frac16,\qquad
y_u=-\frac23,\qquad
y_d=\frac13,\qquad
y_L=-\frac12,\qquad
y_e=1,
\label{eq:hypercharge_values}
\end{equation}
and these are pairwise distinct.

\section{2. Hypercharge spectral projectors}

Because the eigenvalues \eqref{eq:hypercharge_values} are distinct, the projector onto each block is the Lagrange polynomial
\begin{equation}
\Pi_r(Y_0)
=
\prod_{s\neq r}
\frac{Y_0-y_s\mathbf 1}{y_r-y_s},
\qquad
r\in\{Q,u,d,L,e\}.
\label{eq:Lagrange_projector_again}
\end{equation}
These satisfy
\[
\Pi_r\Pi_s=\delta_{rs}\Pi_r,
\qquad
\sum_r \Pi_r=\mathbf 1.
\]

\section{3. Polynomial reduction of the full pre-EWSB operator}

Let \(\mathcal Y_{\mathrm{pre}}\) be the actual gauge-equivariant pre-EWSB family operator induced from the full defect PDE on
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{chiral}}.
\]
Since it commutes with the gauge action, and since the chiral blocks are separated by distinct hypercharges, one may write
\begin{equation}
\mathcal Y_{\mathrm{pre}}
=
\sum_{r} H_r\otimes \Pi_r(Y_0).
\label{eq:Ypre_projector_sum}
\end{equation}

Because there are exactly five distinct eigenvalues, the same operator can equivalently be expanded as a degree-\(\le 4\) polynomial in \(Y_0\):
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_{\mathrm{pre}}
=
\sum_{n=0}^{4} A_n\otimes Y_0^n,
}
\label{eq:Ypre_poly}
\end{equation}
where each
\[
A_n:\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}\to\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}
\]
is a Hermitian \(3\times3\) family operator.

\section{4. Relation between the five sector operators and the five coefficients}

Evaluating \eqref{eq:Ypre_poly} on the hypercharge-\(y_r\) block gives
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_r
=
\sum_{n=0}^{4} y_r^{\,n} A_n,
\qquad
r\in\{Q,u,d,L,e\}.
}
\label{eq:Hr_from_An}
\end{equation}

Define the Vandermonde matrix
\begin{equation}
V_{rn}=y_r^{\,n},
\qquad
r\in\{Q,u,d,L,e\},\quad n=0,\dots,4.
\label{eq:Vandermonde_def}
\end{equation}
Since the \(y_r\) are distinct, \(V\) is invertible. Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A_n
=
\sum_{r}(V^{-1})_{nr}H_r.
}
\label{eq:An_from_Hr}
\end{equation}

Thus the data \(\{H_r\}\) and \(\{A_n\}\) are equivalent.

\section{5. New nontrivial consequence: criterion for mixing}

Suppose all five coefficient operators commute:
\begin{equation}
[A_m,A_n]=0
\qquad
\forall m,n.
\label{eq:An_commuting}
\end{equation}
Then for any two sectors \(r,s\),
\begin{align}
[H_r,H_s]
&=
\left[\sum_m y_r^{\,m}A_m,\sum_n y_s^{\,n}A_n\right]
\nonumber\\
&=
\sum_{m,n} y_r^{\,m}y_s^{\,n}[A_m,A_n]
=0.
\label{eq:Hr_commuting}
\end{align}

Hence all \(H_r\) are simultaneously diagonalizable.
So they share one common family eigenbasis, and therefore the leading pre-EWSB flavor mixing is trivial:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
[A_m,A_n]=0\ \forall m,n
\Longrightarrow
\text{no intrinsic pre-EWSB family misalignment.}
}
\label{eq:no_misalignment_theorem}
\end{equation}

Therefore realistic flavor mixing requires
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\exists\, m,n \text{ such that } [A_m,A_n]\neq 0.
}
\label{eq:mixing_requires_noncommuting}
\end{equation}

This is the first genuine microscopic flavor criterion beyond the structural theorem chain.

\section{6. Finite matrix extraction}

Choose a family basis
\[
\phi_1,\phi_2,\phi_3\in\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}.
\]
Then
\begin{equation}
(M_r)_{ij}
=
\langle \phi_i,H_r\phi_j\rangle
=
\sum_{n=0}^{4} y_r^{\,n}\,\langle \phi_i,A_n\phi_j\rangle.
\label{eq:Mr_from_An}
\end{equation}
So the actual microscopic task is reduced to the five family-space matrices
\[
A_0,\ A_1,\ A_2,\ A_3,\ A_4.
\]

\section{Final Summary}

The pre-EWSB flavor problem is sharper than five unrelated block operators.

Because the five hypercharges are distinct, the full gauge-equivariant family operator can be written as
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_{\mathrm{pre}}
=
\sum_{n=0}^{4} A_n\otimes Y_0^n.
}
\]
Then the five chiral block operators are
\[
\boxed{
H_r
=
\sum_{n=0}^{4} y_r^{\,n} A_n,
\qquad
r\in\{Q,u,d,L,e\}.
}
\]

Thus the next real microscopic task is not to derive five unrelated \(H_r\), but to derive the five family-space coefficient operators
\[
\boxed{
A_0,\ A_1,\ A_2,\ A_3,\ A_4
}
\]
from the full TECT defect PDE.

Moreover, realistic flavor mixing requires at least two noncommuting coefficients:
\[
\boxed{
\exists\,m,n:\ [A_m,A_n]\neq0.
}
\]
Otherwise all sectoral family operators are simultaneously diagonalizable and there is no intrinsic family misalignment.

\section{Extraction formula for the hypercharge-polynomial family operators}

\section{Microscopic pre-EWSB decomposition}

Let the full pre-EWSB microscopic operator on
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}\otimes \mathcal F_{\mathrm{SM}}
\]
be
\begin{equation}
\mathbb H_{\mathrm{pre}}
=
\mathbb H_0+\delta\mathbb H_{\mathrm{pre}}.
\label{eq:Hpre_split}
\end{equation}
Assume gauge equivariance with respect to the unbroken pre-EWSB gauge action.
Since the five hypercharges are distinct, any gauge-equivariant operator can be written uniquely as a polynomial in \(Y_0\) of degree at most \(4\):
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta\mathbb H_{\mathrm{pre}}
=
\sum_{n=0}^{4}\delta\mathbb H^{(n)}\otimes Y_0^n,
}
\label{eq:dHpre_poly}
\end{equation}
where each
\[
\delta\mathbb H^{(n)}
\]
acts only on the family/defect part.

\section{Protected family projector}

Let
\[
P:\mathcal H_{\mathrm{full}}\to \mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}
\]
be the projector onto the protected three-dimensional family subspace, and
\[
Q=1-P.
\]
Let \(E_*\) be the protected energy of the degenerate family triplet under the unperturbed operator \(\mathbb H_0\).

Then the exact Feshbach effective operator is
\begin{equation}
\mathcal Y_{\mathrm{pre}}(E)
=
P\mathbb H_{\mathrm{pre}}P
+
P\mathbb H_{\mathrm{pre}}Q
\bigl(E-Q\mathbb H_{\mathrm{pre}}Q\bigr)^{-1}
Q\mathbb H_{\mathrm{pre}}P.
\label{eq:Feshbach_pre_exact}
\end{equation}

Expanding around \(E_*\) and keeping the standard second-order Schur term gives
\begin{equation}
\mathcal Y_{\mathrm{pre}}
=
P\,\delta\mathbb H_{\mathrm{pre}}\,P
-
P\,\delta\mathbb H_{\mathrm{pre}}\,Q
\bigl(Q\mathbb H_0Q-E_*\bigr)^{-1}
Q\,\delta\mathbb H_{\mathrm{pre}}\,P
+\cdots.
\label{eq:Ypre_SW_again}
\end{equation}

\section{Induced polynomial coefficients \(A_n\)}

Substituting \eqref{eq:dHpre_poly} into \eqref{eq:Ypre_SW_again}, one obtains
\begin{equation}
\mathcal Y_{\mathrm{pre}}
=
\sum_{n=0}^{4}A_n\otimes Y_0^n
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A_n
=
P\,\delta\mathbb H^{(n)}\,P
-
\sum_{a+b=n}
P\,\delta\mathbb H^{(a)}\,Q
\bigl(Q\mathbb H_0Q-E_*\bigr)^{-1}
Q\,\delta\mathbb H^{(b)}\,P
+\cdots,
}
\label{eq:An_master}
\end{equation}
where the sum runs over \(a,b\in\{0,1,2,3,4\}\) such that \(a+b=n\), and higher-order terms may generate contributions with degree \(>4\), which can always be reduced back to degree \(\le 4\) by the minimal polynomial of \(Y_0\).

Thus the five family operators \(A_n\) are not independent ansatz objects:
they are directly induced by the five hypercharge-polynomial components \(\delta\mathbb H^{(n)}\) of the microscopic defect perturbation.

\section{Finite matrix form in a family basis}

Choose a family basis
\[
\phi_1,\phi_2,\phi_3\in \mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}.
\]
Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(A_n)_{ij}
=
\langle \phi_i,A_n\phi_j\rangle,
\qquad
i,j=1,2,3.
}
\label{eq:An_matrix}
\end{equation}
Equivalently,
\begin{align}
(A_n)_{ij}
&=
\langle \phi_i,\delta\mathbb H^{(n)}\phi_j\rangle
-
\sum_{a+b=n}\sum_{\mu\in Q}
\frac{
\langle \phi_i,\delta\mathbb H^{(a)}\mu\rangle
\langle \mu,\delta\mathbb H^{(b)}\phi_j\rangle
}{
E_\mu-E_*
}
+\cdots.
\label{eq:An_explicit}
\end{align}

This is the actual extraction formula to be evaluated from the defect PDE.

\section{Sector reconstruction}

For each pre-EWSB chiral block
\[
r\in\{Q,u,d,L,e\},
\]
with hypercharge \(y_r\), the sectoral family operator is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_r
=
\sum_{n=0}^{4} y_r^{\,n} A_n.
}
\label{eq:Hr_from_An_again}
\end{equation}
So once \(A_0,\dots,A_4\) are known, all five blocks are known automatically.

\section{Minimal criterion for realistic flavor mixing}

Suppose all \(A_n\) commute:
\[
[A_m,A_n]=0
\qquad
\forall m,n.
\]
Then every \(H_r\) is a polynomial in one commuting family of Hermitian operators, hence all \(H_r\) are simultaneously diagonalizable.
Therefore there is no intrinsic pre-EWSB family misalignment.

So a necessary condition for realistic flavor mixing is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\exists\,m,n\in\{0,1,2,3,4\}
\text{ such that }
[A_m,A_n]\neq0.
}
\label{eq:noncommuting_necessary}
\end{equation}

\section{What is actually left}

The genuine remaining microscopic task is therefore:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{derive the kernels }\delta\mathbb H^{(0)},\delta\mathbb H^{(1)},\delta\mathbb H^{(2)},\delta\mathbb H^{(3)},\delta\mathbb H^{(4)}
\text{ from the full TECT defect PDE.}
}
\label{eq:dHn_open}
\end{equation}
Once these are known, one computes \(A_n\) from \eqref{eq:An_master}, reconstructs \(H_r\) via \eqref{eq:Hr_from_An_again}, and then proceeds to EWSB splitting and eventual CKM/PMNS extraction.

\section{Final summary}

The pre-EWSB flavor problem is reduced to one polynomial family operator:
\[
\delta\mathbb H_{\mathrm{pre}}
=
\sum_{n=0}^{4}\delta\mathbb H^{(n)}\otimes Y_0^n.
\]

Its effective protected-sector reduction is
\[
\mathcal Y_{\mathrm{pre}}
=
\sum_{n=0}^{4}A_n\otimes Y_0^n,
\]
with
\[
A_n
=
P\,\delta\mathbb H^{(n)}\,P
-
\sum_{a+b=n}
P\,\delta\mathbb H^{(a)}\,Q
(Q\mathbb H_0Q-E_*)^{-1}
Q\,\delta\mathbb H^{(b)}\,P
+\cdots.
\]

Then the five pre-EWSB chiral block operators are
\[
H_r=\sum_{n=0}^{4} y_r^{\,n}A_n,
\qquad
r\in\{Q,u,d,L,e\}.
\]

Therefore the actual remaining microscopic flavor task is not to guess five unrelated matrices.
It is to derive the five hypercharge-polynomial kernels
\[
\delta\mathbb H^{(0)},\delta\mathbb H^{(1)},\delta\mathbb H^{(2)},\delta\mathbb H^{(3)},\delta\mathbb H^{(4)}
\]
from the full defect PDE.

A necessary condition for realistic flavor mixing is
\[
\exists\,m,n:\ [A_m,A_n]\neq0.
\]
Otherwise all sectoral family operators are simultaneously diagonalizable.

\section{Explicit Vandermonde inversion for the pre-EWSB hypercharge-polynomial family operator}

\subsection{Setup}

Let the pre-EWSB operator be
\begin{equation}
\mathcal Y_{\mathrm{pre}}
=
\sum_{n=0}^{4} A_n\otimes Y_0^n,
\label{eq:Ypre_poly_final}
\end{equation}
acting on
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}\otimes \mathcal F_{\mathrm{SM}}.
\]

For the five pre-EWSB chiral blocks
\[
r\in\{Q,u,d,L,e\},
\]
the hypercharge eigenvalues are
\begin{equation}
y_Q=\frac16,\qquad
y_u=-\frac23,\qquad
y_d=\frac13,\qquad
y_L=-\frac12,\qquad
y_e=1.
\label{eq:yr_values_final}
\end{equation}

Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_r
=
\sum_{n=0}^{4} y_r^{\,n} A_n.
}
\label{eq:Hr_from_An_final}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Vandermonde system}

Define
\[
\vec H=
\begin{pmatrix}
H_Q\\ H_u\\ H_d\\ H_L\\ H_e
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\vec A=
\begin{pmatrix}
A_0\\ A_1\\ A_2\\ A_3\\ A_4
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Then
\begin{equation}
\vec H = V \vec A,
\label{eq:VH}
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation}
V=
\begin{pmatrix}
1 & \frac16 & \frac1{36} & \frac1{216} & \frac1{1296}\\
1 & -\frac23 & \frac49 & -\frac8{27} & \frac{16}{81}\\
1 & \frac13 & \frac19 & \frac1{27} & \frac1{81}\\
1 & -\frac12 & \frac14 & -\frac18 & \frac1{16}\\
1 & 1 & 1 & 1 & 1
\end{pmatrix}.
\label{eq:Vmatrix}
\end{equation}
Since the \(y_r\) are distinct, \(V\) is invertible.

\subsection{Explicit inversion}

The inverse relation \(\vec A=V^{-1}\vec H\) is:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A_0
=
\frac{36}{25}H_Q
-\frac{3}{25}H_u
-\frac{3}{5}H_d
+\frac{4}{15}H_L
+\frac{1}{75}H_e
}
\label{eq:A0_explicit}
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A_1
=
-\frac{18}{25}H_Q
+\frac{24}{25}H_u
+\frac{21}{10}H_d
-\frac{34}{15}H_L
-\frac{11}{150}H_e
}
\label{eq:A1_explicit}
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A_2
=
-\frac{288}{25}H_Q
-\frac{21}{25}H_u
+\frac{93}{10}H_d
+\frac{16}{5}H_L
-\frac{7}{50}H_e
}
\label{eq:A2_explicit}
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A_3
=
-\frac{54}{25}H_Q
-\frac{108}{25}H_u
+6H_L
+\frac{12}{25}H_e
}
\label{eq:A3_explicit}
\end{equation}
with vanishing \(H_d\)-coefficient.

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A_4
=
\frac{324}{25}H_Q
+\frac{108}{25}H_u
-\frac{54}{5}H_d
-\frac{36}{5}H_L
+\frac{18}{25}H_e
}
\label{eq:A4_explicit}
\end{equation}

Thus the five coefficient operators are exactly reconstructed from the five chiral block family operators.

\subsection{Converse reconstruction}

Conversely,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_Q=A_0+\frac16A_1+\frac1{36}A_2+\frac1{216}A_3+\frac1{1296}A_4
}
\label{eq:HQ_from_A}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_u=A_0-\frac23A_1+\frac49A_2-\frac8{27}A_3+\frac{16}{81}A_4
}
\label{eq:Hu_from_A}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_d=A_0+\frac13A_1+\frac19A_2+\frac1{27}A_3+\frac1{81}A_4
}
\label{eq:Hd_from_A}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_L=A_0-\frac12A_1+\frac14A_2-\frac18A_3+\frac1{16}A_4
}
\label{eq:HL_from_A}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_e=A_0+A_1+A_2+A_3+A_4.
}
\label{eq:He_from_A}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Minimal source criterion for realistic mixing}

Suppose there exist only two independent family-space Hermitian generators \(B_0,B_1\) such that
\[
A_n=\alpha_n B_0+\beta_n B_1.
\]
Then all five sector operators satisfy
\[
H_r=c_r B_0+d_r B_1
\]
for suitable coefficients \(c_r,d_r\).

If
\[
[B_0,B_1]=0,
\]
then all \(H_r\) commute and are simultaneously diagonalizable.
Hence there is no intrinsic pre-EWSB family misalignment.

Therefore a necessary condition for realistic flavor mixing is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the microscopic defect PDE must generate at least two noncommuting family Hermitian sources.}
}
\label{eq:two_noncommuting_needed}
\end{equation}

Equivalently, in the \(A_n\)-language,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\exists\,m,n\in\{0,1,2,3,4\}:\ [A_m,A_n]\neq0.
}
\label{eq:An_noncommuting_final}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

The hypercharge-polynomial flavor reduction is now completely explicit.

The five pre-EWSB sector operators and the five polynomial coefficients are related by an invertible Vandermonde system.
In particular,
\[
A_0,\dots,A_4
\]
are exact linear combinations of
\[
H_Q,\ H_u,\ H_d,\ H_L,\ H_e,
\]
and vice versa.

Therefore the actual microscopic flavor task is not to guess five unrelated blocks.
It is to determine how many independent Hermitian family-space sources the full TECT defect PDE generates.

A necessary condition for realistic mixing is that at least two of those sources fail to commute.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{First nontrivial hypercharge truncation and the minimal flavor-misalignment criterion}

\subsection{Short Korean explanation}

Instead of starting with the full degree-\(4\) hypercharge polynomial, the sharpest first microscopic test is the affine truncation
\[
\mathcal Y_{\rm pre}^{(1)}=A_0\otimes \mathbf 1 + A_1\otimes Y_0.
\]
In this truncation, every pre-EWSB chiral block is of the form
\[
H_r=A_0+y_rA_1.
\]
Because the five hypercharges \(y_r\) are distinct, intrinsic family misalignment is present if and only if
\[
[A_0,A_1]\neq 0.
\]

\section{1. Setup}

Let
\[
\mathcal H_{\rm fam}\cong \mathbb C^3
\]
be the common family Hilbert space, and let the pre-EWSB chiral one-family decomposition be
\[
\mathcal F_{\rm SM}=Q\oplus L\oplus u^c\oplus d^c\oplus e^c.
\]
The primitive hypercharge eigenvalues are
\[
y_Q=\frac16,\qquad
y_u=-\frac23,\qquad
y_d=\frac13,\qquad
y_L=-\frac12,\qquad
y_e=1.
\]

\section{2. First nontrivial truncation}

Consider the minimal nontrivial pre-EWSB operator ansatz
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal Y_{\rm pre}^{(1)}
=
A_0\otimes \mathbf 1
+
A_1\otimes Y_0,
}
\label{eq:Ypre_affine}
\end{equation}
where
\[
A_0,A_1:\mathcal H_{\rm fam}\to\mathcal H_{\rm fam}
\]
are Hermitian \(3\times3\) family operators.

Then on the hypercharge-\(y_r\) block one gets
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H_r=A_0+y_rA_1,
\qquad
r\in\{Q,u,d,L,e\}.
}
\label{eq:Hr_affine}
\end{equation}

Explicitly,
\begin{align}
H_Q &= A_0+\frac16 A_1,\\
H_u &= A_0-\frac23 A_1,\\
H_d &= A_0+\frac13 A_1,\\
H_L &= A_0-\frac12 A_1,\\
H_e &= A_0+A_1.
\end{align}

\section{3. Exact commutator theorem}

For any two blocks \(r,s\),
\begin{align}
[H_r,H_s]
&=
[A_0+y_rA_1,\ A_0+y_sA_1]\\
&=
y_s[A_0,A_1]-y_r[A_0,A_1]\\
&=
(y_s-y_r)[A_0,A_1].
\label{eq:HrHs_comm}
\end{align}

Since the five hypercharges are pairwise distinct,
\[
y_s-y_r\neq 0
\qquad
(r\neq s).
\]
Therefore:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
[A_0,A_1]\neq 0
\iff
[H_r,H_s]\neq 0
\text{ for every }r\neq s.
}
\label{eq:comm_equiv}
\end{equation}

Equivalently:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
[A_0,A_1]=0
\iff
\text{all five }H_r\text{ are simultaneously diagonalizable.}
}
\label{eq:simdiag_equiv}
\end{equation}

\section{4. Minimal flavor criterion}

Hence, in the first nontrivial truncation, realistic intrinsic pre-EWSB family misalignment is present if and only if
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
[A_0,A_1]\neq 0.
}
\label{eq:minimal_mixing_criterion}
\end{equation}

This is both necessary and sufficient within the affine truncation \eqref{eq:Ypre_affine}.

\section{5. Microscopic meaning}

So the immediate microscopic question is no longer:
\[
\text{``what are all }A_0,\dots,A_4\text{?''}
\]
The genuinely first useful question is:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Does the full TECT defect PDE generate a nontrivial }Y_0\text{-odd family source }A_1
\text{ that fails to commute with }A_0?
}
\label{eq:microscopic_question_A0A1}
\end{equation}

If yes, then intrinsic pre-EWSB flavor misalignment already exists at the first nontrivial hypercharge order.

\section{6. Matrix extraction}

Choose a family basis \(\phi_1,\phi_2,\phi_3\).
Then
\[
(A_0)_{ij}=\langle \phi_i,A_0\phi_j\rangle,
\qquad
(A_1)_{ij}=\langle \phi_i,A_1\phi_j\rangle.
\]
The five block matrices are then obtained immediately from \eqref{eq:Hr_affine}.

Thus the next finite microscopic task is:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{extract }A_0\text{ and }A_1\text{ from the full defect PDE and evaluate }[A_0,A_1].
}
\label{eq:next_finite_task}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

The first nontrivial pre-EWSB flavor truncation is
\[
\mathcal Y_{\rm pre}^{(1)}=A_0\otimes \mathbf 1 + A_1\otimes Y_0.
\]
Then all five chiral block family operators are
\[
H_r=A_0+y_rA_1.
\]

Because the five hypercharges are pairwise distinct, one has the exact equivalence
\[
\boxed{
[A_0,A_1]\neq0
\iff
\text{intrinsic pre-EWSB family misalignment exists.}
}
\]

Therefore the next genuinely nonredundant microscopic flavor calculation is:
\[
\boxed{
\text{derive }A_0\text{ and }A_1\text{ from the full TECT defect PDE and test whether }[A_0,A_1]\neq0.
}
\]
